DISPOSITION: Reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a vehicle that was stopped. Defendant was searched and charged with possession of marijuana. He filed a motion to suppress and contended that the search was not based on a reasonable articulable suspicion. The motion was denied, and defendant pleaded guilty. It adopted a bright line rule and held when the police stopped a vehicle, all of the occupants of that vehicle were seized and had standing to challenge the stop. OUTCOME: The court found that the trial court erred when it denied defendant's motion to suppress, reversed defendant's conviction for marijuana possession, and remanded.

State v. Pultz


No. 94-2806


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


206 Wis. 2d 112; 556 N.W.2d 708; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 116


September 25, 1996, Oral Argument  
December 20, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of a decision of the Court of Appeals. Reported at: 197 Wis. 2d 955, 543 N.W.2d 867 (Ct. App. 1995). UNPUBLISHED. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: ROBERT W. LANDRY.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and cause remanded for a new contempt hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner protester sought review of a Court of Appeals (Wisconsin) decision affirming a remedial contempt order of the circuit court, which found the protester in contempt for violating a permanent injunction enjoining him and others from engaging in particular activities at city medical clinics.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, the state and city, initiated contempt proceedings against the protester pursuant to Wis. Stat. 785.03(1). At the contempt hearing, the protester objected on the basis that he desired counsel, but claimed that he had been unable to obtain an attorney because he was jailed on unrelated charges. Nevertheless, the hearing proceeded and the protester was found in contempt. The circuit court ordered the protester jailed after he refused to take an oath that he would not violate the injunction and he failed to pay a forfeiture. However, the contempt order was reversed on appeal. Because the protester's personal liberty was threatened by imprisonment in the remedial contempt action, the court held that the circuit court was required by constitutional due process to have advised the protester of his right to appointed counsel if he could not afford one. The court explained that before the circuit court proceeded on a contempt motion, it was required to advise any pro per contemnor of the risk of imprisonment, of the right to counsel, and the right to appointed counsel if indigent, and the court was to allow a reasonable time for an attorney to be obtained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the circuit court finding contempt and remanded the matter to the circuit court for a new hearing on the contempt motion.

State v. McMaster


No. 95-1159-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


206 Wis. 2d 30; 556 N.W.2d 673; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 106


October 31, 1996, Oral Argument  
December 13, 1996, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals. Reported at: 198 Wis. 2d 542, 543 N.W.2d 499 (Ct. App. 1995). PUBLISHED. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Waukesha. JUDGE: J. MAC DAVIS.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin) that affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss based on its finding that the criminal prosecution of defendant for Wis. Stat. §§ 346.63, 366.65 after the administrative suspension of his driving privileges was allowed under the Double Jeopardy Clause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant received a citation for operating while under the influence of an intoxicant. He had prior drunk driving violations and was charged with a criminal offense. His driving privileges were administratively suspended for six months. The trial court denied defendant's motion to dismiss because it found that Wis. Stat. § 343.305 was remedial in purpose and did not constitute punishment for double jeopardy purposes. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that whether a statute was considered punishment was a finding of constitutional law and was an issue of law. Defendant bore the burden of overcoming the strong presumption that the statute did not subject a person to multiple punishment. In a case of first impression, the court found that the criminal prosecution of defendant after the administrative suspension of his driving privileges was permitted because the primary purpose of Wis. Stat. § 343.305 was remedial and did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause. The legislature intended the statute to be a remedial civil sanction and there were no aspects of the statute that were so punitive in effect or nature as to render the overall purpose to be one of punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

50 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lohmeier


No. 94-2187-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


205 Wis. 2d 183; 556 N.W.2d 90; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 103


September 10, 1996, Oral Argument  
November 29, 1996, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals. Reported at: 196 Wis. 2d 432, 538 N.W.2d 821 (Ct. App. 1995). COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Walworth. JUDGE: James L. Carlson.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which reversed defendant's convictions for two counts of homicide by operation of a vehicle while under the influence of an intoxicant under Wis. Stat. § 940.09(1)(a) (1991-1992).


OVERVIEW: Defendant struck and killed two women with his car while they were walking. Defendant left the scene but later returned and admitted to police that he had hit the women. Defendant failed a field sobriety test and was convicted of homicide under § 940.09(1)(a) and hit and run causing death under Wis. Stat. §§ 346.67, 346.74(5). The appellate court reversed defendant's convictions under Wis. Stat. § 940.09(1)(a), and the court reversed the appellate court's decision. The court concluded that the victims' conduct could be the basis of the § 940.09(2) affirmative defense. The court held that the § 940.09 affirmative defense and the contributory negligence rule of Wis. Stat. § 939.14 were not inconsistent concepts. The court found that it was not reasonably likely that the challenged instructions misled the jury into thinking that it could not consider the women's actions in relation to the affirmative defense. The court held that the trial court did not violate defendant's due process rights. However, the court recommended that the Criminal Jury Instruction Committee adopt a bridging instruction detailing the relationship between contributory negligence and the affirmative defense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision that had reversed defendant's convictions for two counts of homicide by operation of a vehicle while under the influence of an intoxicant.

 

51 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Kara B. by Albert v. Dane County


No. 94-1081 & 94-2908


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


205 Wis. 2d 140; 555 N.W.2d 630; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 100


September 4, 1996, Oral Argument  
November 25, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals. Reported at: 198 Wis. 2d 24, 542 N.W.2d 777 (Ct. App. 1995). COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Dane. JUDGE: Mark A. Frankel/Gerald C. Nichol.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a county and its officials, sought review of a decision of the Wisconsin Court of Appeals. The decision held that petitioners were not entitled to qualified immunity from a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 lawsuit for damages that was filed by respondent foster children after they were sexually abused in their foster care home. The decision also held that the officials' conduct was to be assessed on a professional judgment standard.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners contended that they were entitled to qualified immunity because the constitutional right of foster children to safe and secure placement in a foster home was not clearly established in 1989, when the foster children were abused. They also argued that the scope of the constitutional duty to provide a foster child with safe and secure placement was measured by a deliberate indifference standard, not a professional judgment standard. The court affirmed the lower appellate court's decision. The court held that (1) petitioners were not entitled to qualified immunity because the foster children had a clearly established constitutional right under the Due Process Clause to safe and secure placement in a foster home in 1989, and (2) the appropriate scope of the public officials' constitutionally imposed duty to place foster children in a safe and secure environment was measured by the professional judgment standard.


OUTCOME: The lower appellate court's decision, which held that the county officials were not entitled to qualified immunity from the wards' lawsuit because they were accused of violating a clearly established right, and that their conduct was to be assessed on a professional judgment standard, was affirmed.

State v. Miller


Case No. 94-0159


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


202 Wis. 2d 56; 549 N.W.2d 235; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 77


May 29, 1996, Oral Argument  
June 19, 1996, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals. Reported at: 196 Wis.2d 238, 538 N.W.2d 573 (Ct. App. 1995). PUBLISHED. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Clark. JUDGE: MICHAEL W. BRENNAN.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order of a court of appeals (Wisconsin), which held that Wis. Stat. § 347.245 (1993-1994) was unconstitutional as applied to defendants. The circuit court had imposed forfeitures on defendants, Amish individuals, for failing to comply with § 347.245 by not displaying a red and orange triangular slow-moving vehicle (SMV) emblem on their horse-drawn buggies.


OVERVIEW: Defendants asserted that their religious convictions did not allow them to display the SMV symbol because the fluorescent red and orange colors were too loud and bright. As an alternative, all the buggies involved were equipped with a red lantern and white reflective tape outlining the perimeter of the rear of the buggy. The court of appeals held that Wis. Stat. § 347.245 (1993-1994) was unconstitutional as applied to defendants because the state had not demonstrated that the emblem was the least restrictive means of furthering the state's interest in traffic safety. On appeal, the court affirmed, but based its holding on the guarantees contained in Wis. Const. art. I, § 18 right to worship according to the dictates of conscience. After noting that there was no dispute as to the sincerity of defendants' belief, the court applied the compelling interest/least restrictive alternative standard. The court found that the state failed to demonstrate that public safety on the highways could not be served by defendants' less restrictive alternative. An expert witness for defendants had testified that the white reflective tape provided superior perception to the SMV emblem.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the holding in favor of defendants.

 

53 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Joni B. v. State


Nos. 95-2757-OA, 95-2758-OA


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


202 Wis. 2d 1; 549 N.W.2d 411; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 73


May 3, 1996, Oral Argument  
June 13, 1996, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Rights declared.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, indigent parents of children subject to protection or services actions and judges, commenced an original action seeking a declaratory judgment on the constitutionality of 1995 Wis. Laws 27, § 2442v, which provided that courts could not appoint counsel for any party other than the child in child protection proceedings under Wis. Stat. § 48.13.


OVERVIEW: The Petitioners claimed that the amendment was unconstitutional on the grounds that it violated Wisconsin's separation of powers doctrine and the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution. The court held that the amended statute's flat prohibition on appointment of counsel for anyone other than the child in child protection proceedings clearly intruded upon the authority of the judiciary, as well as unreasonably burdened and substantially interfered with the judicial branch's inherent power to appoint counsel in order to effect the efficient administration of justice. The court further held that the amended statute impermissibly interfered with an inherent authority of the judiciary and, therefore, was violative of the separation of powers doctrine embodied in the state constitution. The court recognized that the legislature had a legitimate interest in its efforts to manage public funds responsibly, but concluded that, in some cases, the state's pecuniary interests would not outweigh the interest shared by the state and the parent in a just and accurate result which would require the equal contest of counseled adversary proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that Wis. Stat. § 48.23(3), as amended by 1995 Wis. Laws 27, § 2442v, violated both the Wisconsin doctrine of separation of powers and the Due Process Clause of the United States Constitution, granted the petitioners' request for relief and declared the amendment contained in 1995 Wis. Laws 27, § 2442v, void.

Libertarian Party v. State


No. 95-3114-OA


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


199 Wis. 2d 790; 546 N.W.2d 424; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 43


January 11, 1996, Oral Argument  
April 9, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Declaratory judgment granted and injunctive relief denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state sought to build a baseball park under its authority pursuant to the Stadium Act (Act), 1995 Wis. Laws 56. Petitioner political party filed a declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of the Act and also filed a motion for injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The political party claimed that the Act violated Wis. Const. art. IV, § 31, because it provided for income, franchise and property tax exemptions that were beneficial to a professional baseball team. The court rejected this argument because the exemptions in the Act applied to the district and its bonds not to the professional baseball team. The political party also argued that the Act violated Wis. Const. art. IV § 31(6) because it constituted an invalid special or private tax law. The court also rejected this argument concluding that the Act contained classifications that were open, germane, and related to the true differences between the entities being classified. Further, the court found that the Act was clearly designed to serve a public purpose and would provide direct advantages or benefits to the public at large. The court found that the Wisconsin legislature had expressly declared that the formation of local baseball park districts would serve a statewide public purpose by encouraging economic development and tourism, by reducing unemployment and by bringing needed capital into the state for the benefit and welfare of people throughout the state.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Stadium Act was constitutional and denied the political party's action for injunctive relief.

Brandmiller v. Arreola


No. 93-2842


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


199 Wis. 2d 528; 544 N.W.2d 894; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 19


November 28, 1995, Oral Argument  
March 13, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals affirming an order of the circuit court. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: ROBERT J. MIECH. Reported at: 189 Wis. 2d 215, 525 N.W.2d 353 (Ct. App. 1994) PUBLISHED.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a class of ticketed motorists, sought review of a decision of the Wisconsin Court of Appeals. The decision affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee municipalities, in the motorists' challenge to the constitutionality of anti-cruising ordinances that the municipalities passed.


OVERVIEW: The ordinances defined cruising as driving a motor vehicle past a designated traffic control point more than twice in any two-hour period during a designated time span. Each member of the class was ticketed for cruising. They contended that the ordinances were unconstitutional because they violated their fundamental right to travel, and were also overbroad. The lower appellate court's decision was affirmed. The court held that the ordinances were constitutionally valid. The court found that, under the intermediate level of scrutiny standard, (1) the cruising ordinances were reasonable time, place, and manner restrictions on the right of localized intrastate travel, (2) the municipalities' interests in ensuring public safety and reducing the significant congestion caused by cruising were significant, and (3) the ordinances were limited in their scope to locations undisputedly affected by the cruising problem, and they left open ample alternative routes to get about town without difficulty. The court also found that the ordinances were not unconstitutionally overbroad.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower appellate court, which held that the municipalities' ordinances that prohibited cruising were constitutional, was affirmed.

Association of State Prosecutors ex rel. Feiss v. Milwaukee County


No. 93-3329


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


199 Wis. 2d 549; 544 N.W.2d 888; 1996 Wisc. LEXIS 20


November 1, 1995, Oral Argument  
March 13, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals affirming a judgment of the circuit court. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: LAURENCE GRAM. Reported at: 189 Wis. 2d 291, 525 N.W.2d 768 (Ct. App. 1994) PUBLISHED.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Milwaukee County and its pension board (county), sought review of a decision of a court of appeals (Wisconsin), which ordered the county to transfer employer contributions made on behalf of county prosecutors from the Milwaukee County Employee Retirement System (County Plan) to the Wisconsin Retirement System (State Plan), pursuant to Wis. Stat. § 978.12(5)(c)5.


OVERVIEW: Under Wis. Stat. § 978.12(5)(c)5, the county was required to transfer employer contributions from the County Plan to the State Plan. The county refused to make the transfer on the ground that it would have misappropriated funds held in trust exclusively for the benefit of vested employees and retirees. The trial court granted mandamus to respondents and ordered the county to comply with § 978.12(5)(c)5. The court of appeals affirmed. On appeal, the court held that transfer of funds from the County Plan to the State Plan pursuant to Wis. Stat. § 978.12(5)(c)5 took property without due process of law.The court reasoned that vested employees and retirees had protectable property interests in their retirement trust funds which the legislature could not simply confiscate under the circumstances. Although the court conceded that legislative modifications were necessary in certain limited situations, the court found no such necessity in the instant case because the County Plan was neither insolvent nor in fiscal distress.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and held that the transfer of funds from the County Plan to the State Plan took property without due process of law.

State v. Akins

SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN

October 6, 1995, Submitted on Briefs  
February 1, 1996, FILED

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction by the Circuit Court for Brown County (Wisconsin) for one count of armed burglary, as party to the crime. Defendant claimed that Wis. Stat. § 970.03 (1993-94) denied him equal protection of the laws by employing different procedural standards for criminal defendant's charged under single or multiple count complaints.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for armed burglary, as party to the crime and the court affirmed. Defendant challenged his conviction claiming that Wis. Stat. § 970.03 (1993-94) denied him equal protection of the laws by employing different procedural standards for criminal defendant's charged under single or multiple count complaints. The court also rejected defendant's contention that the prosecutor erroneously charged in the information the same crime that the commissioner found there was no probable cause for. The court reasoned that the commissioner had exceeded his authority, which was only to was to determine if there was probable cause to bind defendant over for trial. The commissioner found there was probable cause to bind defendant over for trial, but not for the charge of armed robbery.

State v. Carpenter


Nos. 94-1898, 94-2024


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


197 Wis. 2d 252; 541 N.W.2d 105; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 122


September 5, 1995, Oral Argument  
December 8, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from orders of the circuit court for Dane County, Mark A. Frankel, Judge. APPEAL from an order of the circuit court for Sauk County, James Evenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and cause remanded. Reversed and cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The action was before the court on certification by the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin). The State challenged trial court decisions that determined Wisconsin's Sexually Violent Commitments statute, Wis. Stat. ch. 980 (1993-94), was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The State contended that that ch. 980 did not violate the Double Jeopardy or Ex Post Facto Clauses of the Wisconsin and United States Constitutions. In affirming, the court held that the principle purpose of ch. 980 was the protection of the public and the treatment of convicted sex offenders who were at a high risk to re-offend in order to reduce the likelihood that they would engage in such conduct in the future. The court found that those were significant nonpunitive and remedial purposes. According to the court, defendant failed to show that the principal purpose of ch. 980 was punishment, retribution, or deterrence. Therefore, the court concluded that defendants failed to meet their burden to overcome the strong presumption in favor of constitutionality. The court also determined that ch. 980 was not an ex post facto law. According to the court, the sanction under ch. 980 bore a rational connection to the purposes of the legislation. Finally, the court held that the trial court properly concluded that there was probable cause that one defendant was a sexually violent person.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial courts' orders that determined Wisconsin's Sexually Violent Person Commitments statute was unconstitutional, affirmed one trial court's order finding probable cause that one defendant was a sexually violent person, and remanded.

The cases were before the court upon certification by the appellate court.


OVERVIEW: The offenders were convicted of sex crimes. The State sought civil commitment pursuant to ch. 980, which authorized the civil commitment of persons, previously convicted of a sexually violent offense, who currently suffered from a mental disorder that predisposed them to repeat such acts. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a public interest group and certain state legislators, brought a declaratory judgment action against respondents, the governor and the Secretary of the Wisconsin Department of Administration, challenging the governor's partial veto power.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners brought a declaratory judgment action against respondents challenging the governor's partial veto power. The issue presented was whether the Wisconsin Constitution, empowering a governor to approve an appropriation bill "in part," permitted the governor to strike a numerical sum appropriated in the bill and to insert a different, smaller number as the appropriated sum. The court held that the governor had the authority to exercise partial veto by striking a numerical sum appropriated in a bill and inserting a different, smaller amount. The court found that the part of § 153 of the 1993 Executive Budget Bill passed by the legislature, but partially vetoed by the governor by writing in a smaller number properly, was vetoed pursuant to Wis. The court held that, by virtue of the passage by the legislature and the governor's partial veto of § 153 in accordance with the Wisconsin Constitution, § 153, as partially vetoed, had the force and effect of law. The court refused to determine the validity of the other provisions of the 1993 Executive Budget Bill that were vetoed by the governor.


OUTCOME: The court entered a declaration of rights but denied the relief sought by petitioners regarding their challenge to the governor's partial veto power.

Henes v. Morrissey


No. 93-3116


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


194 Wis. 2d 338; 533 N.W.2d 802; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 94


April 5, 1995, Oral Argument  
June 27, 1995, FILED

DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:

64 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Pritzlaff v. Archdiocese of Milwaukee


No. 93-1846


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


194 Wis. 2d 302; 533 N.W.2d 780; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 99


April 26, 1995, Oral Argument  
June 27, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied February 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1015.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: JOHN G. BARTHOLOMEW. Reported at: 188 Wis. 2d 79, 524 N.W.2d 647 (Ct. App. 1994) UNPUBLISHED.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant archdiocese appealed the judgment of the court of appeals (Wisconsin), which reversed an order of the trial court dismissing all claims brought by plaintiff parishioner that arose out of an alleged relationship between the parishioner and a priest, and which remanded the parishioner's negligent hiring, training, and supervision claim for trial.


OVERVIEW: The parishioner brought suit against the archdiocese and the priest alleging that, 27 years earlier, the priest coerced her into a six-year sexual relationship, and that she consequently suffered severe emotional distress. The court of appeals reversed the trial court's dismissal of the parishioner's claims. The court held that the court of appeals erred in doing so. The court held that the trial court was correct in determining that the claims were barred by the three-year statute of limitations of Wis. Stat. § 893.54. The statute of limitations was not tolled by the judicially-created "discovery rule," which provided that a cause of action did not accrue until a plaintiff discovered, or with reasonable diligence should have discovered, that she had suffered actual damage due to wrongs committed by a particular, identified person. The parishioner knew the identity of the alleged tortfeasor and could have alleged a complete cause of action against that priest at the time the relationship ended. Sound public policy required that the discovery rule not be extended to apply to the facts of the case because of the potential for fraudulent or stale actions.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed. The order remanding the case for trial of the negligent supervision claim was reversed.

 

65 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Holtzman v. Knott (In re H.S.H-K)


No. 93-2911


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


193 Wis. 2d 649; 533 N.W.2d 419; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 75


October 12, 1994, Oral Argument  
June 13, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the circuit court for Dane county, George A.W. Northrup, circuit court judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part; and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parent sought review of an order from the Circuit Court for Dane County (Wisconsin), that dismissed her petition for custody of or visitation rights to appellee mother's child. Appellant asserted that appellee was unfit and unable to care for the child, and that compelling circumstances required a change of custody, pursuant to Wis. Stat. § 767.24(3) (1991-92). The issue was also raised whether appellant could seek visitation rights.


OVERVIEW: Following an order that dismissed appellant parent's petition for custody of or visitation rights to the biological child of appellee mother, appellant sought review of the decision. Appellant's asserted that compelling circumstances required a change of custody under Wis. Stat. § 767.24(3) (1991-92). Appellant also sought visitation rights to the child. The court on review, concluded that appellant did not raised a triable issue requiring a transfer of custody, and therefore affirmed the dismissal of the custody action commenced under Wis. Stat. § 767.24 (1991-92). Further, it was found that appellant could not petition for visitation under Wis. Stat. § 767.245 (1991-92). The court reversed the order in respect to the visitation rights of appellant. It was reasoned that a circuit court could have determined whether visitation with appellant was in the child's best interest if appellant first proved that she had a parent-like relationship with the child; and if it was proved a significant triggering event by demonstrating that appellee interfered substantially with the child's relationship with appellant, and that appellant petitioned the court promptly after the interference.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing appellant parent's petition for custody was affirmed. The court reversed and remanded the order dismissing the petition for visitation rights. The court held that a circuit court could have determined whether visitation with appellant was in the child's best interest if appellant proved that she had a parent-like relationship with the child and that appellee mother had interfered with the child's relationship with appellant.

 

66 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


City of Milwaukee v. Kilgore


No. 92-0949


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


193 Wis. 2d 168; 532 N.W.2d 690; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 74


April 4, 1995, Oral Argument  
June 7, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: JOHN E. MC CORMICK. Reported at: 185 Wis. 2d 499, 517 N.W.2d 689 (Ct. App. 1994). PUBLISHED



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, members of a class of individuals who had their drivers' licenses suspended for failure to pay fines imposed for non-traffic-related municipal ordinance violations, challenged a decision of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin) that reversed a circuit court decision that had held that municipal court judges had no statutory authority to order such suspensions and that, if they did, Wis. Stat. §§ 800.09, .095 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The appeal consolidated one petitioner's challenge to his conviction for disorderly conduct with the remaining petitioners' class action against respondents, Secretary of Transportation, municipal judges, and city. The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals. Although Wis. Stat. § 343.30(5) prohibited any drivers' license suspensions not expressly listed therein and did not include suspensions pursuant to Wis. Stat. ch. 800, the court harmonized the two conflicting provisions by noting that the specificity of ch. 800 gave it precedence over the more general powers granted by Wis. Stat. § 343.30(5). It concluded that the legislature intended to authorize license suspensions for the violation of municipal ordinances. Suspensions imposed under Wis. Stat. ch. 800 could be purged by payment of fines and were reasonably related to the reason for the suspension. Wis. Stat. §§ 800.09, .095 were not unconstitutional because they were rationally related to the state's interest in securing compliance with court orders and they were a valid exercise of the police power. The fines were not excessive in violation of the federal and state constitutions because they could be purged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals that had reversed the circuit court decision that had held that municipal court judges had no statutory authority to order the suspension of petitioners' drivers' licenses for their failure to pay fines imposed for non-traffic-related municipal ordinance violations, and that had held that statutes providing such authority were unconstitutional.

State v. Randall


No. 93-0219-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


192 Wis. 2d 800; 532 N.W.2d 94; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 50


November 10, 1994, Oral Argument  
May 10, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the order of the Circuit Court for Waukesha County, Joseph E. Wimmer, Circuit Court Judge. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Waukesha. JUDGE: JOSEPH E. WIMMER. ON CERTIFICATION FROM THE COURT OF APPEALS.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Circuit Court for Waukesha County (Wisconsin) that denied his motion to challenge the constitutionality of a statute that kept an insanity acquittee, who was no longer mentally, confined solely on the grounds that he was a threat to himself or others.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first degree murder and burglary. By pleading guilty by reason of insanity to murder and not burglary, defendant was subjected to a statutory scheme which confined an insanity acquittee, who was no longer mentally ill, solely on the grounds that the individual was a threat to himself or others. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of this statute. The lower court denied his motion. He appealed from the denial. The court certified the question of whether the statutory scheme violated the Due Process Clause of the United States Constitution. The court affirmed, holding that due process was not violated when an insanity acquittee, who has committed a criminal act, was confined in a state mental health facility for so long as he or she was considered dangerous, provided that the commitment did not exceed the maximum term of imprisonment which could have been imposed for the offense charged. The court determined that there was a reasonable relationship between the commitment and the purposes for which the individual was committed and, therefore, that insanity acquittees were treated in a manner consistent with the purposes of their commitment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of defendant motion to challenge the constitutionality of a statute that kept an insanity acquittee, who was no longer mentally, confined solely on the grounds that he was a threat to himself or others. The court held that the statute did not violate the due process rights because there was a reasonable relationship between the commitment and the purposes for which the individual was committed.

Martin by Scoptur v. Richards


No. 91-0016


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


192 Wis. 2d 156; 531 N.W.2d 70; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 47


February 1, 1994, Oral Argument; November 8, 1994, Oral Argument  
May 4, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a published decision of the Court of Appeals affirming in part and reversing in part a judgment of the Circuit Court of Jefferson County, Arnold K. Schumann, Judge. REVIEW OF A DECISION OF THE COURT OF APPEALS REPORTED AT: 176 Wis. 2d 340, 500 N.W.2d 691 (Ct. App. 1993) PUBLISHED.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, parents and their severely injured daughter, filed suit against defendants, doctors, insurance companies, a hospital, and the Wisconsin Department of Health & Social Services, alleging negligence of the doctors, staff and hospital in treating the daughter. The Circuit Court of Jefferson County (Wisconsin) granted the doctor's motion to dismiss relative to informed consent. The court of appeals reversed, and the doctors appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parents filed suit on behalf of their severely injured daughter following her treatment at a hospital for head injuries after she rode her bicycle into the back of a dump truck. The doctor failed to inform the father that a CT scanner was available to diagnose the extent of his daughter's head injuries. The circuit court granted the doctor's motion to dismiss relative to the informed consent claim. The court of appeals reversed and remanded. The court held that (1) one of the doctors knew that the daughter's condition was more serious than a simple concussion, (2) the jury's decision on informed consent was proper, (3) the failure of the verdict to contain a question on cause with respect to the issue of informed consent was not fatally defective because such a question was waived by the parties, (4) that the circuit court was correct in determining that Wis. Stat. § 448.30, was inapplicable to a minimally involved doctor, and (5) the retroactive cap on noneconomic damages was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the jury's decision on informed consent, reversed the court of appeals' holding that the failure of the verdict to contain a question on cause was fatally defective, reversed the court of appeals finding that the informed consent statute was applicable to a doctor. The court also found that the retroactive cap on noneconomic damages was unconstitutional and the parents and their daughter were entitled to their full award.

State ex rel. Friedrich v. Circuit Court


No. 94-2095-W, No. 94-2637-W


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


192 Wis. 2d 1; 531 N.W.2d 32; 1995 Wisc. LEXIS 40


February 21, 1995, Oral Argument  
April 18, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Dane & Bayfield.



DISPOSITION: No. 94-2095-W: Petition for Writ of Mandamus. Rights Declared. No. 94-2637-W: Petition for Supervisory Writ. Rights Declared.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorneys sought a writ of mandamus for compensation as a result of their positions as court-appointed guardians ad litem by respondent Circuit court for Dane County (Wisconsin). The petition was consolidated with a petition for supervisory jurisdiction related to compensation of a court-appointed special prosecutor.


OVERVIEW: Two cases related to the compensation of court appointed guardians ad litem and attorneys were consolidated for consideration. The conflict involved two separate fee schedules, one established by Wis. Sup. Ct. R. 81.01, 81.02, and one by Wis. Stat. § 977.08(4m), which established a lower rate. The two attorneys were appointed as guardian ad litem for clients and compensated at the lower statutory rate sought a writ of mandamus. The attorneys alleged that the statutes were an unconstitutional infringement of the state supreme court's authority to appoint and compensate counsel. The appellate court found that the issue of compensation for appointed attorneys was an area in which the judiciary and the legislature shared authority. The court held that the judiciary's power was inherent in its ability to appoint counsel, and the legislature had authority to govern the use of public funds. The court found that the supreme court rules were valid, and that the statutes were also facially constitutional. The court issued a new rule to cover the compensation of appointed counsel.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Wisconsin supreme court rules were valid, but that the statutes in question were also facially constitutional.

Northern States Power Co. v. Bugher


No. 92-1634


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN

November 9, 1994, Oral Argument  
January 18, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals. REPORTED AT: 179 Wis. 2d 849, 514 N.W.2d 723 (Ct. App. 1993). UNPUBLISHED. Source of APPEAL COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Dane. JUDGE: JACK F. AULIK.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner power company sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which affirmed the trial court's decision to dismiss its claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, challenging the constitutionality of Wis. Stat. § 71.04(5)(d)1 (1975), because the power company had failed to exhaust its administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: The power company donated lands located in Wisconsin to the federal government, deducting the value of the lands from its franchise taxes, pursuant to Wis. Stat. § 71.04(5)(d)1 (1975). The state department of revenue (DOR) determined that the deductions were not proper. The power company exhausted its administrative and judicial remedies, before filing a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 against state officials in their individual capacities, challenging the constitutionality of the state tax. The trial court found that the action was barred by res judicata. On review, the court found that the power company and the DOR were parties to the prior litigation and the officers of the DOR were in privy with the DOR. As the power company's underlying claim at every stage of the proceeding was that it was entitled to a deduction for state income tax purposes based on its gift of land to the federal government, the court found that there was an identity of claims. The court held that the power company's failure to raise its claim regarding the constitutionality of the state tax during the initial proceedings meant that it was barred under the claim preclusion doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the power company's claim challenging the constitutionality of a state tax.

State v. Thiel


Nos. 93-1724-CRFT and 93-1725-CRFT


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


188 Wis. 2d 695; 524 N.W.2d 641; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 128


October 13, 1994, Oral Argument  
December 20, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of an unpublished decision of the Court of Appeals affirming the judgment of the Circuit Court of Waupaca County, John P. Hoffmann, Judge. Reported at: 180 Wis.2d 472, 514 N.W.2d 56 (Ct. App. 1993) UNPUBLISHED



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, an ex-convict, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which affirmed a decision of the circuit court prohibiting him from possessing firearms.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a felony in 1970. Eleven years later, in 1981, the Wisconsin Legislature enacted Wis. Stat. § 941.29, which prohibited convicted felons from possessing firearms. Defendant challenged the statute as it applied to him, arguing that it retroactively punished him for the felony he committed before the statute was enacted, and therefore violated the Ex Post Facto Clause of the Wisconsin Constitution. Upon review, the court found defendant's arguments without merit. The statute was not enacted with the intent to punish convicted felons and as such was not an ex post facto law as applied to him. Rather, the restriction on convicted felons' ability to possess firearms came about incident to firearm regulation out of concerns of public safety.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the affirmance of the judgment prohibiting defendant from possessing firearms.

State v. Black


No. 92-2325-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


188 Wis. 2d 639; 526 N.W.2d 132; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 125


September 9, 1994, Oral Argument  
December 14, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an Order of the Circuit Court of Milwaukee County, Arlene D. Connors, Judge, on certification from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed an order from the Circuit Court of Milwaukee County (Wisconsin), which dismissed a feticide charge based on Wis. Stat. § 940.04(2)(a). The circuit court held that the statute did not proscribe the conduct that defendant was accused of committing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant destroyed the life of his unborn quick child by violently assaulting his wife five days prior to her anticipated delivery date. The baby had bled to death after the placenta was detached from his mother's uterine wall. Defendant was charged under Wis. Stat. § 940.04(2)(a). The circuit court dismissed the count. On appeal, the court held that the statute plainly proscribed the defendant's alleged action. The court determined that the assaultive behavior was proscribed by § 940.04(2)(a). Defendant contended that the statute was not intended to apply to feticide but to apply only in the context of consensual medical abortions. He cited the title of the statute, "abortion," as evidence. He also argued that § 940.04(2)(a) could not be enforced against him because it was impliedly repealed when the legislature enacted § 940.15 in response to Roe v. Wade. The court disagreed. There was no question that the legislature had the power to pass a feticide statute. Wis. Stat. § 940.04(2)(a) was not an abortion statute. It proscribed the intentional criminal act of feticide: the intentional destruction of an unborn quick child presumably without the consent of the mother.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that dismissed defendant's feticide charge and remanded for further proceedings.

Lewis v. Sullivan


No. 92-3141


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


188 Wis. 2d 157; 524 N.W.2d 630; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 120


October 24, 1994, Submitted on Briefs  
December 7, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW OF A DECISION OF THE COURT OF APPEALS Reported at: 178 Wis. 2d 877, 506 N.W.2d 427 (Ct. App. 1993). UNPUBLISHED. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Dane. JUDGE: GEORGE NORTHRUP.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The circuit court dismissed the complaint of plaintiff, an inmate at a correctional facility, seeking relief against defendants, department of corrections employees, for their alleged improper seizures of personal items sent to him, on the ground that the plaintiff had not complied with the notice of injury requirement of Wis. Stat. § 893.82 (1983-84). The court of appeals affirmed the order of the circuit court and plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: Two legal questions were presented on appeal: (1) did the complaint state a claim upon which relief in the form of replevin, a declaratory judgment, or injunctive relief could be granted and (2) did plaintiff's failure to comply with the notice of injury requirement of Wis. Stat. § 893.82(3) (1983-84) bar relief for replevin, declaratory relief, or injunctive relief. Plaintiff's complaint alleged that defendants' policies and procedures relating to seizure of property were an unconstitutional deprivation of property without due process and had a "chilling" effect on the exercise of constitutional rights. The court concluded that the complaint stated and a claim for relief and that plaintiff sought monetary damages for detention of the property under the replevin statute, as well as declaratory and injunctive relief. Because plaintiff did not comply with § 893.82(3), his claim under the replevin statute for damages for detention of the property failed. The injunctive and declaratory relief accorded plaintiff were adequate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and remanded the cause to the circuit court to determine the merits of the claims for declaratory and injunctive relief.

State v. Heft


No. 92-2938-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


185 Wis. 2d 288; 517 N.W.2d 494; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 96


May 25, 1994, Oral Argument  
June 23, 1994, Filed

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin) that affirmed an order denying her motion for postconviction relief after she was convicted of homicide by intoxicated use of a vehicle in violation of Wis. Stat. § 940.09(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that she was denied her right to due process when the circuit court refused to require a witness to invoke his Fifth Amendment privilege in the presence of the jury and that Wis. Stat. § 905.13 created an unconstitutional distinction between criminal and civil proceedings by prohibiting comment upon or inference from a claim of privilege in a criminal but not in a civil trial. The court affirmed the order, holding that defendant was afforded a meaningful opportunity to present a complete defense and that § 905.13 did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of either the Wisconsin or United States Constitution. The court reasoned that defendant introduced substantial evidence supporting her defense that even if she had not been under the influence of an intoxicant, the victim would have died anyway because of the witness's conduct. The court found a rational basis for the distinction between civil and criminal treatment of a witness's invocation of the Fifth Amendment privilege and the inferences that could have been drawn from such an invocation, explaining that the consequences of a criminal conviction were must more sever than in a civil action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

State v. Iglesias


Nos. 92-2437-CR, 92-2679-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


185 Wis. 2d 117; 517 N.W.2d 175; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 94; 42 A.L.R.5th 909


May 26, 1994, Argued  
June 22, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of a judgment of the circuit court for Dane County, George A. W. Northrup, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, accused and her sureties, sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court for Dane County (Wisconsin), which denied their motions for postconviction relief and applied the accused's bail money to fines imposed against her pursuant to Wis. Stat. § 969.03(4), after the accused was convicted of soliciting a child for prostitution and being a party to the crimes of child enticement and second degree sexual assault of a child.


OVERVIEW: The accused was arrested and charged with multiple offenses stemming from her role in engaging two girls to work as prostitutes. The trial court set bail, which was paid by the sureties. On appeal, the accused and her sureties contended that the trial court erroneously exercised its discretion when imposed a fine without first assessing the accused's ability to pay. The court held: (1) the trial court properly exercised its discretion in imposing the fine and considered the accused's health, the gravity of her crimes, her past criminal behavior, her character, and the need to deter would-be offenders from similarly exploiting minors; (2) the trial court properly reasoned that the accused had the ability to pay the judgment because the money posted for bail, irrespective of its source, was conclusively presumed to be the accused's money; and (3) § 969.03(4) did not violate the Eighth Amendment because § 969.03(4) had no bearing upon why bail was set nor in what amount it was set, but rather was a procedural mechanism by which the trial court could collect fines.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment imposing fines against the accused and ordering that the bail money be used to satisfy the judgment.

State ex rel. Shroble v. Prusener

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant candidate sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which held that an action in quo warranto was available to plaintiff opponent to challenge the results of an election in defendant city based on claimed mistakes in the canvassing process.


OVERVIEW: The opponent brought an action against the candidate and the city in which he claimed that Wis. The court of appeals reversed the judgment of the trial court and held that an action in quo warranto was available to challenge the results of the election. Stat. § 9.01, plainly and unambiguously provided the exclusive remedy for challenging the results of an election based on mistakes in the canvassing process; and (2) the recount statute, as the exclusive remedy, did not violate the opponent's due process and equal protection rights.

Szarzynski v. YMCA, Camp Minikani


No. 92-0089


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


184 Wis. 2d 875; 517 N.W.2d 135; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 87


March 1, 1994, Oral Argument  
June 20, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 30, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: REVIEW of a decision of the Court of Appeals. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: Patricia D. McMahon



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a nonprofit corporation that owned and operated a recreational day-camp (corporation), appealed an order of the Court of Appeals for Milwaukee County (Wisconsin), which reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in its favor based on Wisconsin's Recreational Use Statute, 1983 Wis. Laws 418, § 1, Wis. Stat. § 895.52, in a personal injury action filed by plaintiffs, an injured minor and her mother (camper).


OVERVIEW: The trial court awarded summary judgment in favor of the corporation, on the grounds that the camper's personal injury action was barred by the limited immunity provided under Wis. Stat. § 895.52. However, the appellate court reversed, holding the statute unconstitutional on equal protection grounds. The court reversed the holding of the appellate court and held that the inclusion of nonprofit organizations in Wisconsin's Recreational Use Statute, Wis. Stat. § 895.52, was not a violation of the equal protection clauses of the United States and Wisconsin Constitutions and did not deny the camper due process of law. The court stated that the camper failed to prove that the legislature had acted unreasonably in creating the distinction between non-profit and for-profit owners of recreational facilities, and that this distinction was rationally related to a legitimate government purpose of making more lands available for public recreational use. The court, further, held that the term "nonprofit organization," as defined in the statute, was not ambiguous, and that the trial court had correctly determined that the corporation qualified for immunity under the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and reinstated the judgment of the trial court, which granted summary judgment in favor of the corporation.

State v. Johnston


No. 92-1857-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


184 Wis. 2d 794; 518 N.W.2d 759; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 85


April 27, 1994, Oral Argument  
June 17, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] REVIEW of a decision of the court of appeals. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Winnebago. JUDGE: Robert A. Haase



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision of the court of appeals (Wisconsin), which reversed the circuit court's judgment convicting defendant of selling alcoholic beverages to underage persons in violation of Wis. Stat. § 125.04(1), evading the law by giving away fermented malt beverages in violation of Wis. Stat. § 125.315(1), and a civil forfeiture violation of Wis. Stat. § 125.07(1). The court of appeals found the warrantless search invalid.


OVERVIEW: Undercover agents entered defendant's home, by invitation, and witnessed a party in progress. Defendant requested that the agents pay him money, which the agents understood to be for the alcoholic beverages which were provided. Pursuant to a prearranged signal, backup officers entered the premises and arrested defendant, charging him with selling alcoholic beverages to underage persons and evading the law by giving away fermented malt beverages. The circuit court convicted defendant of those offenses and the jury also found him guilty of a civil forfeiture violation. The court of appeals reversed, concluding that the warrantless search and seizure were invalid, and remanded to the circuit court. On appeal, the supreme court reversed, holding that the search, seizure, and arrest were valid. Defendant invited the undercover officers and the civilian agent into his home to attend the party, and thus their entry was lawful. Both the events witnessed by the undercover officers and the items which were later seized were within the officers' plain view and subject to seizure. No jurisdictional defects were created by the joinder of the civil forfeiture and criminal misdemeanor charges.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the court of appeals' order reversing defendant's conviction for selling alcoholic beverages to underage persons and evading the law.

 

81 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Irby v. Macht


No. 90-2662


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


184 Wis. 2d 831; 522 N.W.2d 9; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 86


April 26, 1994, Oral Argument  
June 17, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 30, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Review if a decision of the Court of Appeals. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Dane. JUDGE: Richard J. Callaway



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed a dismissal of his 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims that respondent corrections employees violated his due process rights by placing him in segregation and reducing good-time credits earned without providing him the procedural protections mandated by the Wisconsin Administrative Code, DOC regulations. The Court of Appeals (Wisconsin) affirmed because he failed to exhaust administrative remedies. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: Wisconsin Administrative Code, DOC 303.68 defined a loss of good time as a major penalty, and it could have been imposed only after the inmate was found guilty of a disciplinary rule. Therefore, after the state provided an inmate with good-time credits, subject to forfeiture after being found guilty of serious misconduct, the determination whether the misconduct occurred became critical, and procedural due process requirements had to be satisfied. Thus, the inmate had a protected liberty interest in the retention of his good-time credits. Similarly, the inmate had a protected interest in his remaining in the general population because the Wisconsin Administrative Code required certain due process procedures be satisfied before his placement in segregation. However, the inmate's due process rights were not violated because he was given an adequate post-deprivation remedy to the random, unauthorized acts that violated the regulations. The court observed that the inmate had already, once, successfully challenged the state's actions, by certiorari review to the circuit court, and again he sought review of state's reimposition of the same discipline based upon the same challenge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the inmate's claims for due process rights violations, but for a different reason. The court concluded that the adequate provision of post-deprivation remedies for the corrections employees' random and unauthorized acts, provided the inmate with due process.

 

82 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Georgina G. v. Terry M. (In the Interest of Angel Lace M.)


Nos. 92-1369, 92-1370


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


184 Wis. 2d 492; 516 N.W.2d 678; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 70


March 2, 1994, Oral Argument  
June 8, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the circuit court for Brown County, Richard J. Dietz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, same sex partners, sought review of an order from the Circuit Court for Brown County (Wisconsin), which denied the petitioners' request to terminate respondent father's parental rights and to allow a petition for adoption.


OVERVIEW: The child's mother and father divorced, and the child lived with petitioners. The father had no contact with the child. Petitioners filed an action to terminate the father's parental rights and for adoption of the child by the mother's partner. The father consented to termination of his parental rights. No party requested termination of the mother's parental rights. The trial court found that the adoption was in the child's best interest but denied the petition on the ground that the mother's partner was not competent to adopt the child and that the child was not competent to be adopted under Wis. Stat. §§ 48.81 and 48.92. Petitioners claimed on appeal that the adoption should have been granted in the best interest of the child and that the application of §§ 48.81 and 48.92 violated their due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court rejected petitioners' claims. The court ruled that the child was not eligible for adoption because the rights of both parents had not been terminated. The court also ruled that the child's best interest in an adoption proceeding was not a protected liberty interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of petitioners' request for termination of the father's parental rights and for adoption of the child. The court found that the child was not eligible for adoption and that denial of the adoption did not violate the child's constitutional rights.

 

83 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


State v. Oimen


No. 90-2927-CR


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


184 Wis. 2d 423; 516 N.W.2d 399; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 68


January 12, 1994, Argued  
June 7, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Review of a decision of the court of appeals. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Dane. JUDGE: GEORGE NORTHRUP



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a ruling of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin) affirming his conviction on a charge of felony murder pursuant to Wis. Stat. § 940.03 as a party to a crime and on attempted armed robbery pursuant to §§ 943.32(1)(b) and (2), and 939.32(1) also as a party to a crime. Defendant challenged the application of § 940.03 applied to a defendant whose co-felon was killed by the intended felony victim as well as a related jury instruction.


OVERVIEW: Although defendant masterminded a plan by which he and two co-felons would rob a bookie, he remained in the car while his two co-felons entered the bookie's home. The plan went awry when the bookie fatally shot one of the co-felons. Defendant was convicted of felony murder and appealed. When the appellate court sustained the trial court's rulings, defendant sought further review. The court affirmed. Under the circumstances, defendant was properly charged with and convicted of felony murder based on the bookie's killing of his co-felon because even though it was the bookie who shot the co-felon, defendant's conduct was a "substantial factor" in bringing about that result. The court also held that conduct during immediate post-crime flight was culpable and that the evidence supported the jury's determination that defendant's conduct was a substantial factor in the co-felon's death. After noting that felony murder is a strict liability offense and that conviction did not require proof of any mental state, the court specifically declared that § 940.03 was constitutional. It also provided directions as to the elements that were to be included in valid jury instructions under § 940.03.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appeals court sustaining defendant's conviction on various charges, including felony murder.

 

84 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Millers Nat'l Ins. Co. v. City of Milwaukee


No. 91-2536


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


184 Wis. 2d 155; 516 N.W.2d 376; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 61


March 1, 1994, Argued  
June 1, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: REVIEW of a decision of the court of appeals. COURT: Circuit. COUNTY: Milwaukee. JUDGE: Patrick J. Madden



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which reversed a circuit court decision granting the city summary judgment in a subrogation action filed by plaintiff insurer. The reversal was granted on the basis that Wis. Stat. § 66.189 required the city to provide uninsured motorist insurance (UM) coverage on its vehicles and that the insurer was entitled to pursue its claim of contractual subrogation.


OVERVIEW: The insurer provided UM coverage to its insured, a city police officer. The officer was injured during the course of his employment when an uninsured motorist struck his motorcycle. The insurer paid the claim under its UM coverage, then filed an action to collect the city's statutorily required UM coverage limits. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court. The court held that § 66.189 was mandatory, not directory, and that the city's responsibility to provide UM coverage was the same whether it was self-insured or otherwise insured. Because the city was considered as any other insurer for the purposes of the statute, the city was also considered as any other insurer for the purposes of either equitable or contractual subrogation. The court rejected the city's arguments that: (1) § 66.189 did not require the city to provide coverage when the insured was covered by a private insurance policy, (2) the insurer did not qualify for subrogation because the self-insured city was not the tortfeasor, (3) subrogation in favor of the insurer went against equity and public policy, and (4) violated the public purpose doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court to reverse the circuit court's dismissal of the insurer's subrogation action against the city. The court remanded the cause for further proceedings not inconsistent with the court's opinion.

State v. Thiel


No. 92-0794-CR


Supreme Court of Wisconsin


183 Wis. 2d 505; 515 N.W.2d 847; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 53


October 12, 1993, Oral argument  
May 13, 1994, Decided  
May 13, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from orders of the Circuit Court for Sheboygan County, Gary Langhoff, Circuit Judge.



DISPOSITION: By the Court. -- The orders of the circuit court are affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the cause is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of order suppressing statements in favor of appellee defendant who cross-appealed denial of motions with regard to preliminary hearing deficiencies on certification to the Circuit Court for Sheboygan County (Wisconsin).


OVERVIEW: Appellant state sought review of order suppressing inculpatory statements in favor of appellee defendant to a charge of felonious exhibition of, and attempt to exhibit material harmful to children under Wis. Stat. § 948.11. Appellee cross appealed denied motion to dismiss on grounds of an alleged overbreadth of the statute. The court concluded, in response to the certified question, defendant's statement "do you think I need an attorney" was insufficient to invoke his right to counsel. The court explained such a statement was equivocal and insufficient to invoke the right to counsel but where such an equivocal inquiry occurs, all interrogation must cease until the ambiguity is resolved. The court noted it reviewed de novo questions of constitutional fact. The court observed defendant signed a second waiver after pausing momentarily following his inquiry. The court found defendant was not coerced into signing the waiver forms and was informed of his rights on two separate occasions and unequivocally waived rights to an attorney by such conduct. The court reversed the order suppressing defendant's statements, and affirmed in all other respects, finding the statute constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed order suppressing appellee defendant's inculpatory statements, concluding in response to a certified question that defendant's statement inquiring whether he needed an attorney was equivocal and insufficient to invoke the right to counsel and his signed waiver of rights was voluntary. The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motion to dismiss because the obscenity statute was constitutional.

Edward Kraemer & Sons v. Sauk County Bd. of Adjustment


No. 91-0886


Supreme Court of Wisconsin


183 Wis. 2d 1; 515 N.W.2d 256; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 46


November 30, 1993, Submitted on briefs  
May 4, 1994, Decided  
May 4, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of a decision of the court of appeals. Reversing 175 Wis. 2d 168, 499 N.W.2d 211 (Ct. App. 1993).



DISPOSITION: By the Court. -- Decision reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Sauk County Board of Adjustment, sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which reversed a judgment from the Sauk County circuit Court that affirmed the board's denial of a special exception permit sought by appellee landowner to extract minerals on a 40-acre parcel of land in Sauk County.


OVERVIEW: The landowner, a mining company, bought the 40-acre parcel of land, which was zoned for agricultural use and was located near a land formation known as the Baraboo bluffs. The landowner sought a special exception permit to conduct mining activities including excavating, removing, and processing crushed stone. Opponents emphasized the geological and aesthetic significance of the bluffs and the adverse effect on recreation and tourism in the area. By a unanimous vote, the Board denied the permit application provisionally, while giving the landowner an opportunity to supplement the application with a plan to preserve a portion of the bluffs. The trial court affirmed the decision, but the appellate court reversed. The appellate court held that if the Board determined that the bluffs needed to be protected, then it was the Board's duty to formulate such a protection plan. The court disagreed and reinstated the trial court judgment, holding that the public health, safety, and welfare standard was broad enough to allow the Board to demand such a plan. The court held that an applicant for a special exception permit had the burden of showing that the use met the ordinance standards.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reinstated.

In re Judicial Disciplinary Proceedings Against Dreyfus


No. 93-1660-J


Supreme Court of Wisconsin


182 Wis. 2d 121; 513 N.W.2d 604; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 37


February 3, 1994, Submitted on briefs  
April 6, 1994, Decided  
April 6, 1994, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judge sought review of a decision of a judicial conduct panel (Wisconsin), which heard the judicial disciplinary complaint of the judicial commission, concluded that the judge engaged in misconduct, and recommended that the judge be suspended from judicial office for 30 days.


OVERVIEW: The judge stipulated to the allegations of the commission's complaint. The panel recommended a 30-day suspension. On review, the court held that the judge's aggravated and/or persistent failure to promptly decide two cases for more than 15 months violated the standard of the Code of Judicial Ethics, Wis. Sup. Ct. R. 60.01(4). The judge's preparation, signing, and submission of false and misleading certificates of the status of pending cases and his willful misleading of the deputy chief judge and the judicial commission investigator violated Wis. Sup. Ct. R. 60.13. The court found that the judge, if overburdened, evaded the mechanisms, including the case status certification rule, designed to assist him. The court determined that the nature of that misconduct, which included attempting to conceal his failure to promptly decide cases and submitting false certifications, taking into account mitigating factors, warranted a 15-day suspension. In mitigation, the court considered that the judge was relatively new, was in one of the busiest courts, was highly esteemed, admitted the falsity of the statements to the investigator within a week of making them, and expressed remorse.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the judge suspended from his office without compensation and prohibited him from exercising his powers or duties for 15 days.

 

88 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Wisconsin Power & Light Co. v. Public Serv. Comm'n


No. 91-1096


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


181 Wis. 2d 385; 511 N.W.2d 291; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 15


September 7, 1993, Oral argument  
February 8, 1994, Decided  
February 8, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of a decision of the Court of Appeals, 171 Wis. 2d 553, 492 N.W.2d 159 (Ct. App. 1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the court of appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent commission appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which affirmed a trial court's judgment reversing respondent's determination that petitioner utility company had imprudently administered a coal contract and overcharged its customers for electricity for several years, and ordered petitioner to pay millions in penalties because it constituted impermissible retroactive ratemaking.


OVERVIEW: Respondent commission found that petitioner utility company had imprudently administered a coal contract and overcharged its customers for electricity for several years and ordered petitioner to pay millions in penalties. The trial court reversed the holding that the penalty constituted impermissible retroactive ratemaking, and the appellate court affirmed. The court affirmed, concluding that no provision in Wis. Stat. § 196 expressly authorized respondent to order a utility to refund money to its customers when the utility might have acted imprudently. The court concluded that Wis. Stat. § 196.37(1) only allowed respondent to determine and make any just and reasonable rates, tolls, charges, schedules or joint rates to be imposed, observed and followed in the future. The court concluded that respondent's order did not fall within the narrow "extraordinary loss" exception to the rule against retroactive ratemaking. The court concluded that respondent was only to set rates prospectively, without recourse to backward-looking remedies like petitioner's penalty ordered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment affirming the trial court's ruling reversing respondent commission's order penalizing petitioner utility company for imprudently administering a coal contract and overcharging its customers for electricity for several years because the penalty was retroactive rulemaking.

In re Disciplinary Proceedings Against Beaver


No. 91-2159-D


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


181 Wis. 2d 12; 510 N.W.2d 129; 1994 Wisc. LEXIS 11; 58 A.L.R.5th 855



February 2, 1994, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In original proceedings before the Wisconsin Supreme Court, relator Board of Attorneys Professional Responsibility sought to suspend respondent attorney's license to practice of law.


OVERVIEW: The referee had found that the attorney's acts of: (1) threatening to kill an opposing lawyer; (2) striking and pushing that man's vehicle with his own; and (3) making misrepresentations to the Board concerning the attorney's withdrawal from a client's representation constituted misconduct for which the attorney should be suspended. The Board prevailed. Clear and satisfactory evidence supported the referee's findings of fact. The attorney's misrepresentations to the Board during its investigation violated Wis. Sup. Ct. R. 22.07. The attorney's behavior toward the opposing lawyer violated the attorney's oath to abstain from all offensive personality that was codified in Wis. Sup. Ct. R. 40:15. Indeed, under Wis. Sup. Ct. R. 20:8.4(g), it was misconduct to violate the attorney's oath. The offensive personality clause was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The context in which the term "offensive personality" was used and its application to lawyer disciplinary proceedings restricted its meaning to conduct reflecting adversely on a person's fitness as a lawyer. The term was not vague because its context and construction rendered the term understandable to a licensed attorney.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that: (1) the attorney be suspended from the practice of law for 90 days; (2) the attorney pay the costs of the proceedings; and (3) the attorney comply with the duties applicable to persons whose licenses to practice law had been suspended.

91 of 2046 DOCUMENTS


Casteel v. McCaughtry


No. 91-0218


SUPREME COURT OF WISCONSIN


176 Wis. 2d 571; 500 N.W.2d 277; 1993 Wisc. LEXIS 517


March 30, 1993, Oral argument  
June 3, 1993, Decided  
June 3, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of a decision of the Court of Appeals affirming in part and reversing in part an order of the circuit court for Dane County, Judge Robert A. De Chambeau, 168 Wis. 2d 758, 484 N.W.2d 579 (Ct. App. 1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the court of appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent prison officials sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Wisconsin), which held that Wis. Admin. Code § DOC 303.11 provided a liberty interest to appellant inmate in remaining out of temporary lockup (TLU) and that the inmate's failure to file a notice of claim under Wis. Stat. § 893.82 in his declaratory and injunctive relief action, brought pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and state law, did not bar his state law claims.


OVERVIEW: The inmate alleged that he was deprived of his liberty by being removed from the general prison population and placed in TLU on six occasions over five years. He claimed that he was not served with a conduct report prior to placement in TLU, was not given a hearing, and that his status there was not reviewed every seven days as required by Wis. Admin. Code § DOC 303.11. On review, the court found that the Fourteenth Amendment protected liberty interests arising from the Due Process Clause and the laws of the states, that § DOC 303.11 established "substantive predicates" to govern official decisionmaking, and that it did not mandate the outcome to be reached upon a finding that the relevant criteria were met. The court found that the statute left the decision whether to place an inmate in TLU up to the discretion of the officials and that placement in TLU was not mandatory. The court held that § DOC 303.11 did not create a liberty interest. The court ruled that while Wis. Stat. § 893.82 barred claims for injury, damage, or death if not brought within 120 days of an alleged act, it did not bar claims for declaratory relief. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the decision of the court of appeals and held that the inmate could bring his claims for declaratory and injunctive relief even if he did not file a notice of claim within 120 days of the alleged acts. The court reversed the judgment and ruled that the temporary lockup statute did not create a liberty interest for inmates in staying out of temporary lockup.
